Chapter XII.
On Removing Stinging Hairs from the Eye with Forceps When the Hair is Firm.
When there are unnatural hairs, hard and rough, that sting the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine forceps with a delicate curved point, and adjust the ends of it carefully and grip each hair in a subtle manner. And let the length of the fields be one palm in quantity.
And below that, prepare another fine layer briefly, and use the extremities of it if you wish.
And then place the patient's head under a canopy, and position it towards the sun so that your work is visible, for this is a delicate and precise operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the hair that requires removal, and quickly guide it through the eyelid before you extract it from above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair in from above and insert it into an opening, and let there be one, two, or three hairs but no more.
And then pull your hand away so that the hair is pulled out from the opening in the eyelid along with the natural hair.
But if it is pulled from the opening and the hair is not removed, pull the opening to the sides towards the layer you have prepared, so that it releases the eyelid sufficiently.
And look at it with the eye, for now you will pull out the opening and the layer, and tie each one securely, leaving them tied for two or three days until the hair adheres to its place and grows back on that good flesh.
And if the hair is too short, now attach a long hair from the natural hairs to it, and elevate it with that, or leave it until it grows longer over some space, and then elevate it.
Chapter XIII.
On the treatment of an abscess that occurs in the upper eyelid.
When an abscess occurs in the eye and causes contraction of the eyelid when it is closed, either to the right or left side, due to poor healing of a wound, incision, or cautery, it is called leporine. This type of abscess can be either natural or accidental, caused by poor healing of wounds, incisions, or cauterizations, or similar mishandling.
The method for the operation is that you should cut open that consolidation and separate its edges, then place between them a piece of linen cloth, and bind it there until it heals.
And you should not use treatments that dry out the abscess or constrain it; for if you do this, the abscess will return worse than before and will never heal properly.
Use treatments that soften the abscess, such as fenugreek; you can boil the seeds and leaves in water or use juice from dill, which should be mixed with some oil to make it liquid, then apply a cloth soaked in this mixture for treatment.
The goal of your treatment is to ensure that you open any area that has not properly healed according to its original form at the beginning.
And this operation we have described is merely a correction of an abscess that does not return to its original form as it was before.
Chapter XIV.
Of the treatment of ehacrati which is done on the eyelid below.
This ehacrati which is done on the eyelid below, which is truly called ehacrati; and some of it is natural, others are accidental.
The accidental one is caused by plague or by cutting or by cautery or by other similar means.

And this figure is the Greek letter: lambda.
And then remove the flesh there in the amount of the width below it, which dries the eyelid.

Chapter XV.
On the adherence of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation occurs in some people such that the eyelid is adhered above to the sclera, and its movement and natural functions are restricted, then you should introduce a probe below the eyelid, lift it up with it, or extend it to the side.

Chapter XVI.

And both types do not begin except from the larger tear duct, gradually spreading to cover the eye and preventing light and movement of the eye.

Chapter XVII.
On the incision of hemorrhoids and of what is born from flesh tightly joined in the groin.
In some cases in the groins of certain individuals, protruding flesh forms, and it covers the pupil, or approaches or stretches over the eyelids, forcing the eyelids to bulge outward and is assembled like a balustrade's edge.

However, you must be careful to remove all foreign matter from the wound naturally or accidentally using instruments, and do not put your hand in any of these operations until you see that the member can withstand the operation.

Chapter XX.
On the Reduction of the Prominence of the Eye.
When the eye protrudes entirely and no harm or reduction occurs in vision, then you should allow the patient to rest, and afterward apply windy poultices to the head without incision and use proper suction; then apply a liniment made of aloes, sesame, and sarcocolla around the eye; then strengthen the plaster over the eye with powder.

Chapter XXIV.
On the treatment of flesh wounds on the nose.
When on the nose there arise various flesh growths that are attached, some of which have causes similar to a spider's web made of thick threads, or to a sea polyp; and this illness is called polypus because it resembles that sea creature. Chapter XXV.
About the glands naturally found in the extremities of the nose.

And for this reason, you should treat it at the beginning of its appearance and carefully manage everything related to it. Here is the translation:
If it is delayed in its incision until it becomes hardened, then consider whether it has become pusified, hard, of dark color and with little sensation; now do not touch that part with a sharp instrument because there are many instances where one who incises this boil does great harm to the person on whom it was done.
And if the boil is touchable and not of dark color, and there are times when incision is possible in all such cases, then evacuate the pus and incise it without fear, and treat the area with substances that dry and constrain until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
On suturing the nose and lips and ear when continuity is disrupted by plague or similar causes.
Know that when there is disruption of continuity in one of these cartilages, few will undertake such an operation except in some cases.
Therefore, when there is any cause for one of these conditions, consider whether it is a recent plague or the result of blood, and then you should approximate the two sides of the wound with sutures, and subsequently treat it until it heals.
And if the sides of the disruption of continuity are already separated and each is healing well, then it is necessary that you scrape away each part of its apparent edge until blood appears. And then approximate the two sides with sutures and strengthen them, and over them sprinkle powdered dragon's blood and thrisse (a type of powder), and place on top a piece of mastic plaster or another consolidating plaster, and leave it for two days or three, and then remove it and change the plaster, and leave it until the exudates have dried up, and then treat with an ointment until it is healed.

Chapter XXVII.
On the extraction of those causes that cause bleeding in the two lips in the partitions within.
Often in the partitions within the lips of some men, there appear hard swellings similar to fat from birds, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore, you must turn the lips and cut each of those hardness from all sides, and when the incision is made, fill the place with finely shredded wool until the blood is constricted, and then wash it with something astringent and treat the area with water that has hemostatic properties until it heals.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the incision of the flesh that is adhered to the gums.
Often there arises on the gum flesh that is adhered a condition which the ancients called "albulus," then you must suspend it with an arrow or what you have at hand and incise it near its base, allowing the pus to flow out or for the blood to run.
And then apply to the area finely shredded wool or other astringent powders or substances that promote drying.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it again, and cauterize it, for after the cautery it will not return.
Chapter XXIX.
On the extraction of teeth with forceps.
When on the surface of the teeth in the upper and lower jaws and between the adhered gums there are rough edges, sharp points, and sometimes they are blackened and appear green or yellow due to corruption of the gum, and thus the teeth become decayed.
Therefore, make the patient sit between your legs and place his head in your lap, and rasp the teeth in which appear those rough spots and causes similar to sand until nothing of them remains, and do this for the blackness and yellowness and other such matters until they are cleansed.
Therefore, if that is sufficient as at the beginning of the treatment, it is good; but if not, then repeat the treatment on those [teeth] another day and on the second and third until it reaches the final stage in what you wish.
And know that the teeth have need of various tools for rasping and of different shapes according to how they are prepared for your operation. And because the rasp with which the tooth is filed from inside is different from the rasp with which it is rasped between the teeth according to other forms, and these forms of rasps you should prepare those that you will see shortly:
Chapter XXX.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
Carefully treat the tooth of its pain with all gentleness and delay in total extraction, for there is no substitute for it when it is extracted, as it is a substance sublime that excuses the extraction of it.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is painful, for sometimes the pain deceives the patient and seems to be in a healthy tooth, and if you extract that one, the pain will not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted, as we have already seen by the practice of barbers.
Therefore, once you are certain which is the painful tooth, and when you know well, it is necessary that you incise around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it dissolves from the tooth and gums completely, and then remove that with your said instruments or with fine forceps little by little until you have cleared away all of it.
And then use strong pincers for good handling or strong crushing, and place the head of the patient between your knees so that you hold them firmly without hurting them, and then pull out the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause greater pain to the patient.
And if it is not forcibly extracted, then take one of those instruments and place it underneath from all sides with ease and exert effort to remove it as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is broken or decayed, then you must cover that fractured part and secure it with a thin wire so that it does not break further during your constriction on it with pincers.
And you must operate extremely carefully around the incision in the gum all around and guard against your strength so that you do not break it and leave any part behind, and return to the patient for the sake of their well-being so that it is better than the initial pain.
And be careful not to do what some ignorant barbers do in their attempts and audacity regarding the extraction of teeth, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they cause great harm to sick people which is more difficult to cure, and the roots of those teeth are broken completely, or partially, or they extract it along with some bones of the jaw as we have witnessed many times.
And after the extraction let the mouth be washed with either vinegar or water salted.
And if blood flows because of this, just as you would do for similar wounds, then apply some quantity of dust and sprinkle it on the wound, and if that does not suffice, cauterize.
The shape of the forceps and holding them with subtlety at which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be long in extremity, short in handle, and thick, so that they are not bent when using them to work.
And they should be like handles of thick material so that when you grasp them they are not bent, and their extremities should be short.
And they should be made of Indian iron with straight extremities, and at the extremities of those there should be teeth, some of which fit into others and grasp firmly by constriction, and sometimes the extremities of those are in the shape of a bird's beak called ciconia, which is a heron, and they are very strong for constriction.

Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of teeth that protrude beyond the others.
When the teeth that protrude do not follow their natural course and especially when they are in places where they should not be, particularly in women and more so in those who are respected; therefore, you must consider: if a tooth is close to another tooth and it is impossible to file or cover it, then remove that tooth; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument which has the shape of a pig's snout, and it is made of Indian iron with an extremely sharp point.
And when you cut it, do so gradually over several days due to the hardness of the tooth, and ensure that it does not become inflamed or affect other teeth.
And if it is a protruding tooth that can be filed down, then file it with an Indian iron file which has this shape:
And take Indian iron and the hammer and shape it accordingly; and with small points as with a file, on which needles are made, grind the tooth little by little over several days with ease, so that it does not become another crooked tooth or break. And sew that line at the end, and cut that with scissors.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is broken and you use the tongue to speak, now you should file that inside so that the roughness of that piece is smoothed out and evened, and fill it in so that the tongue does not hurt nor the speech be impaired.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the restoration of crooked teeth with gold or silver filling.
When you encounter front teeth that are loose and displaced due to impact or accident, and the patient cannot bite on them when eating, and you have treated them with astringent medicines but your treatment has not been effective, now in order to prevent them from falling out, you should strengthen those that are crooked with gold filling and not silver, because silver changes color and will be noticeable right away, whereas gold never changes.
And the filling should be of such fineness and thickness according to the quantity that can fit between the teeth; the shape of the restoration is that you take the gold and place twice its amount between two healthy teeth, and then build up with two tortuosity shapes of the filling between one or more crooked teeth, until it reaches the surface of the adjacent healthy tooth. And then sew the suturing at the part where you started, and strengthen your work with ease and skill so that it is not crooked.
And let your thread be such that you can pull out the pieces of the tooth without any difficulty.
And then cut off the excess part of the thread with forceps or scissors, and attach it at each place with your fingers, and convert and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that it does not injure the tongue. And then make it as narrow as a Roman coin.
But if it is loose or broken, pull out that thread in such a way over the entire period of time.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the correction of two healthy teeth and of two damaged teeth, as you see them tied:
And when it happens that you return one tooth or two back to their proper places after extraction, and they grow back as we have recommended, and remain in place.
And only skilled artisans and wise people can do this.
And sometimes a cause for using cow bone is given, and it is shaped like a tooth, and placed in the location where the tooth is missing, and it is attached as we have said, and remains and serves in that place for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the cutting of the band that goes around the tongue and prevents speech.
This band that goes beneath the tongue sometimes is natural, with which one is born, or it is accidental and occurs due to an injury that has already healed.
Of the operation in which men open the mouth of the patient, his head being placed in a convenient position; and raise his tongue, and then incise that nerve-band horizontally within which the tongue is loosened from its attachment.
If there is any hardness or swelling in that area, which has become consolidated due to disease, now you should gently cut it open along the same horizontal line where you previously divided the band, so as to dissolve the hard lump.
And be careful not to make a cut in the surface of the flesh, so that you do not divide here an artery and cause a flow of blood.
And then wash the remnants of the incision with cold water or vinegar water or simply clean water; and afterwards place a piece of linen under the tongue; this is done so that the patient will keep something under their tongue each night to prevent it from healing too quickly.
But if blood flow occurs, place over the area powdered vitriol; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the site with a hot cautery that is suitable for this purpose. And then proceed with the treatment along with other causes until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
On the extraction of a sublingual cyst beneath the tongue.
At times, beneath the tongue, there appears a swelling similar to a small abscess that prevents the tongue from performing its natural functions; and it rapidly enlarges until it fills the mouth.
And the operation is to open the mouth of the patient facing the light and examine the swelling.
And if you see it of dark or black color and hard, in which the patient feels no sensation, then do not touch that which is gangrenous.
And if it is inclined towards whiteness, in which there is humidity, then you put the incision into it, and divide that with a thin spatula, and divide it from all parts; and if blood flows during the time of your operation, then pause above three times, which is vitriol, until the blood constricts.
And then return to your work once you have treated it properly, after washing it with vinegar and salt; and treat that with the other methods until it is healed.
Chapter XXXVI.
On the extraction of abscesses from both ears and those that are in the cheek, and other abscesses.
When you encounter an abscess in the cheek area, which are glands that are assembled somewhat together, some of which appear outside and are called ears. When you treat them as we have said in the division and they do not heal, then consider if the abscess is hard, dark in color, with little sensation. At this point, do not touch it with instruments.
And if it is of red color and the root is thick, then do not touch that with instruments due to the flow of blood; rather, let that remain until it has matured, and then either you will remove it, or it will rupture on its own.
And if it is of white color, soft, and the root of it is thin, then that is what should be incised.
In the operation on which you are about to perform work, before your operation, consider whether the abscess from the cautery has healed completely or if it has diminished in any way; and now place the patient facing the sun, with his head towards the east, and breathe into the mouth of the patient, and the assistant takes between his fingers the tongue of the patient below this noble instrument which is of this form:
And let it be made of silver or iron, and let it be thin like a knife.
Then as you press with that on the tongue and the abscess is shown to you, quickly place it in the center and pull it out towards the sides as far as you can. Do the same for any other area for the removal of pus, and then cut that area with an instrument whose form is as follows:
Do the same for any other area for the removal of pus, and then cut that area with an instrument whose form is as follows: and it is similar to forceps, but the ends are curved, so that the opening of each one is in line with the other, sharp, made of iron.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then cut that with a scalpel whose form is as follows: It is sharp on one side and blunt on the other side.
And after you have cut one incision, cut the other in the same manner, and then wash the patient's wound with fresh water.
But if there is bleeding, wash the wound with water decocted from lemon peel or myrtle leaves and similar astringent substances until the flow of blood is stopped, and then treat it until it is healed.
And in some cases, we incise on the gum boil or abscesses and others on those two ears, and incise those as it is said in the incision of two glands similarly.
And I had already treated a woman with an abscess which was born in the gum of her tooth, which abscess tended to have a dark color, little sensation, which had closed the gum of her, and the woman breathed very difficultly, and had prohibited her from eating and drinking water, and she would have reached death if it had waited another day or two.
And the abscess had swollen so much that it had formed two channels inside, through which it discharged through two openings.
Then I decided to act surgically and inserted into one of its cavities, and pulled it out, and most of it came out in large pieces, and then I incised that from which I could reach the other opening, and then I closed its mouth, and compressed its tongue, and then I placed the cavity back into that abscess.
And then I removed a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. And then the gum of that woman healed absolutely, and she began to drink water immediately, and soon after she ate food. Therefore, I did not need to incise that abscess again for a long time, and an abscess succeeded in its place, which was incised before it could prolong.
Then I heated it and cauterized the gum of it, which was an addition to the treatment; and then I walked around and did not know what God would do next with that situation.
Chapter xxxvii.
On incising an abscessed hovel (likely meant to be "boil" or "abscess").
When the discharge from the boil indicates that it is abscessed and elongated, it is then called perpendicular; and if it is located below and is thick and round, it is then called a bubba.
However, when it is cured as we have described in the division and your treatment does not conflict with it, yet sometimes an abscess persists beneath the surface and is subtle, then you should incise the one that is aggregated and round; and if it extends to a certain length and is bloody, dark brown, or black, and is not at the center, then you should attempt the incision cautiously, for there is concern about malformation.
And when you see it according to the method I have described regarding its whiteness and length, make the patient sit down and press with an instrument that reveals its form and location, then place your thumb below it and incise it with one of two instruments mentioned in the procedure for double incisions.
And you should not incise it beyond what is appropriate within the natural limits, for if you incise too much, you may harm its function.
And after the incision, use what we have recommended for the care of double incisions, and treat it until it is healed.
However, if the patient is frightened by the incision, you should proceed with cautery without fear, and the method of cauterization involves only sharp water. Place the head of the patient on your lap, then press with the instrument as described, and take the sharp water mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it to the area causing pain, and place something not too thick or thin over it, and fill the concavity of the instrument whose shape is like this:
Here is the extraction from the cautery in which the medicine is placed as with the cautery of copper alloy, and place the instrument over the wound with the medicine on it, and the patient lies on his back so that saliva or mucus from the mouth does not fall onto the fresh dressing because otherwise it could cause infection in that area.
And then take the medicine and apply it to the uvula for half an hour until you see it become blackened and numb from the bite of the medicine.
And if you wish, take a cloth and wrap it around the end of the cautery, then dip the cloth in the medicine. Insert the cautery with the cloth into a tube so that the cloth adheres to this tube. Do this as many times as needed until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the wound. Then leave it alone until it dries and falls off after about three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, do so, and after the cauterization clean around the wound with a cloth dipped in vinegar, and modify it with what is around the wound from the medicine. Wash it with fresh water, then treat it externally with an ointment, and keep it clean until it heals.
And this particular method of treatment is safer and more effective than incision and the prolonged fear it brings.
In some cases, the uvula is treated with something that is more subtle than cauterization and incision. It is healed through sublimation in this manner: take camphor, hyssop, mint, rue, Arabian saffron, chamomile, and similar herbs, adjust all these causes or some of them in a container, dip them in sour wine, and boil; the vessel should be heated moderately, and at the center of the vessel place a tube upon which is placed an instrument made of reed in this manner: it should be made of silver or reed.
And insert the extremity into the mouth of the patient until the steam reaches the uvula above which the uvula is obscured.
And then proceed with this treatment until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this cure at the beginning of the onset of abscesses, for it would be too harsh on the abscess, and you should not do this, but rather let the abscess declare itself by heat.
But if this instrument is not at hand, now take a cane and attach to the end of it a cloth of wool, so that the mouth of the patient is covered, for the cloth of wool prevents the steam from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter XXXVIII.
On the extraction of spines and what adheres in the flesh and other causes.
Pads adhering to the flesh or fish spine or some other cause.
Then you should remove what is apparent from those, on which your sight rests, and press the tongue with an instrument in front of sunlight, so that it becomes clear to you what is in the flesh.
And what does not appear and is hidden in the flesh, you should make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for the cause attached to the flesh will be easily expelled by vomiting; or have the patient swallow a piece of dry bread or lettuce juice; or take a piece of dried sea sponge, bind it tightly with thread, and have the patient swallow it, for when it reaches the place where spines are lodged, it will then expel them. Take a eel and prepare quicksilver with it, let the interior be emptied and let the preparation be extracted; or take a rod and apply the preparation to it and let the preparation be drawn out.

Chapter XXXIX.
On the extraction of a leech attached to the throat.
When the leech has been treated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and bandages, now look at the patient's throat in the sunlight, since you have retracted the tongue of the patient with the instrument which we have recommended.
Then if you see the leech, remove it gently with your fingertips carefully.
And if it is not possible for you to remove it, now take a concave tube and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron in the cavity of the tube, and do this several times (until the leech dies and falls off), and have the patient abstain from water for the entire day.
Take a flask full of clean water and place the mouth of it over [the wound], and wash the mouth with that; and do not pour out any of that for any reason and change the water once an hour each time by its time, because the leech will take in the blood as soon as it feels the water cold.
But if it is not possible to use what we have recommended with that water, then smoke the wound with cimex or with asafetida by means of the instrument which we have described for smoking the uvula.
Do this, and so it will fall off.
And there is another method of operation in smoking that one, which is to take a vessel containing ignited charcoal and cover the vessel with a cover through the middle of which is a hole; and secure the end of the instrument over this opening; then proceed with the smoking from above; place the wound on the mouth of the tube, and close it so that the smoke does not escape until you know that the smoke has reached the leech, because the leech will fall off immediately.
And if it does not work, then repeat the smoking several times, and prevent the patient from drinking, give salty and dry foods, and do not allow water to be drunk; and it is necessary that with this regimen the treatment be effective.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech from the groin is treated when it is seen; and it resembles forceps as you may see, but it has some curvature. which is placed in the groin, and the end of it resembles the beak of a stork, which is roughened with a file, so that when it grips something, it does not let go.
Chapter XL.
In which is the summary of the speech on the preparation for abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are various types of swellings, in many ways, as will be recalled for each one in this book.
And they differ in their puncture and incision by two methods, one of which is according to the nature of the abscess itself; and also due to what we derive from their humidity.
And the second method is according to the locations in which they occur on the body, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from one occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess occurring in fleshy areas is different from one occurring at joints; each has its treatment by operation.
And there are some abscesses that should not be punctured but left until the pus has properly matured or they have reached completion.
There are other abscesses that should be evacuated when they are crossed, even if they have not matured according to their completion, such as those near joints, because an abscess near a joint can spread inward until you reach what is around it, and the tendons or nerves of that joint may become corrupted due to contraction of that limb; or the abscess is close to a principal member, and if you delay puncturing it while it is not yet matured, it could cause harm to the principal member; or it is near the buttocks, so puncture it before it matures; but do not puncture it when it is raw and incomplete digestion, except for one that is close to the buttocks, to avoid the prepuce of it being elongated into the inner parts of the buttocks, forming a fistula, or causing it to occur at its term without healing. May you know the time for piercing the abscess, and this is when it is ripe.
And you should know that it is ripe when there is an alleviation of the pain and the cessation of the fever, and shortly after a reduction in the redness and pulse, and the sharpness at the head of the abscess, and other signs.
And you should know that the piercing should be made at the place where the pus is deepest in the abscess and coming from the elevation.
And the piercing should always be made along the length of the flesh or body if the abscesses are in the parts of the thighs or arms, and in places where there are tendons, cords of nerves, arteries; and finally, in all other equal areas where there is no reflection; for those that do reflect, use a piercing according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy location, the best time is to delay the piercing until the digestion of the second stage is confirmed, as we have said; for if you pierce it before this, it prolongs the illness of the patient and leads to excessive corruption, and hardens the lips of the wound and its depth.
But some abscesses should be pierced across the grain of the flesh or body due to necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And you should administer a small piercing, and in large abscesses, a larger or wider piercing according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And in other abscesses where the treatment should be hollowed out and incised, when it is done as such, it should be at a time when it has already begun to develop, just like those that occur with excessive boils and buboes.
Of those which are cut open with a three-angled section; and of those in which it is cut open as the figure of an olive leaf, just like the abscesses of the angles.
And of those in which a round section and a lunar section are administered, and similar to these.
But in the case where it is not such as their superficial lion abscesses, you should puncture that with a superficial incision, or just plane it.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected pus and corrupt matter, you should puncture that and do not hesitate to extract all the pus at that time; having extracted part of it, then scrape the abscess on another day, and then extract a larger portion, and continue this process several times according to its degree until it is healed.
And especially if the patient is a vigorous man, or a pregnant woman, or an infant, or an elderly person in frailty, for in these cases the vital spirit is quickly resolved through the evacuation of the pus, and the patient easily recovers, and you should treat them accordingly.
Therefore, guard this chapter with great caution, and when you make the puncture through which you open these abscesses, you must clean the plaques and then consider whether the plaque of the abscess is small or if it requires a flat section; in that case, you should administer some of it or use cork bark. And if the abscess is large, and the incisions from the puncture are many, then you should place wax on each part until they heal.
And if in the abscess a part of the bone is cut away or you have hollowed it out around the circumference, then you should fill it with old cork or thread made from linen cloth, as when you treat ulcers or tumors without moisture; and strengthen that on the third day; and thereafter proceed to care for it according to what is needed among the remedies until it is healed.
If blood flows during the time of your operation, then you should administer water to wash and prepare cloths in which a cloth is placed, and place that over the spot where the blood flows for several times.
If the bleeding continues, then you should administer treatment and cure and powders which I have recommended in many places of this our book and division.
If it is in the time of winter, and the place of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then you should infuse powders in wine and cauterized oil, and place those on the places.
And if it is in summer, and the places are fleshy, then you should place powders soaked in water and oil or in wine and oil, and all of this should be rubbed in until the third day, as we have said, and then you should lance the abscess and cleanse it and administer to its cure what is suitable for it until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the cure of abscesses generally, but according to the method of distinction I have already said about every abscess in what manner is the way of its cure separately by chapters.
Chapter 41.
On the section of abscesses which appear in the neck of the head.
In the neck of the head, small abscesses, and there are various types of nodes which contain membranes that are similar to their formations; and these types vary.
And they are soft, and those contain humidity similar to lime. And of those which contain humidity similar to dust, and resembling them; and of those which are lithic, hard.
And in the section of all, and in the extraction of those there is no fear, as the extraction of those does not oppose the structure.
And the operation in the section of those is that you express them with an instrument whose form approaches this, which is called an entrobes, until you know what it contains.
For if what it contains is humidity, then you should divide that with a section according to its length by making a superficial section according to this form:
And begin the section from the line of .B. to the line of .G. Then, as the humidity is evacuated, you should excise that part which contains the humidity, and do not leave any cause for recurrence, for if any cause remains, it may return.
And then immerse a piece of cloth in Egyptian ointment if it is available, and if not, in water and salt; and fill that area with the dressing and leave it for another day, for in this way you will corrode all that remains of that membrane.
And then apply the piece of cloth with Egyptian ointment again and again if necessary, until you are confident that there is no remaining cause of that membrane; now treat the wound with an ointment until it is healed.
And if an abscess contains hard nodes, then you should incise that according to this form: with a section having the shape of a cross, and place needles between two lips of the incision and scrape it thoroughly from all sides, and exert your strength in extracting the membrane that contains it.
If there is opposition to what I have written about surgery, now you should read what I wrote first.
And similarly, I deal with the treatment and cure of abscesses as we have told you; and the incision for a lithified abscess is easier, because it contains little blood and less moisture.
And sometimes there are certain abscesses in the head of some people who do not have much moisture throughout their body; the cause of this is that I once incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found it as hard as a stone, rough, white, which could not be softened, and if anyone were to strike it, it would hurt them.
However, in other abscesses that occur in the head, this is not the case with this chapter; similarly, for abscesses that affect the heads of children and behind their ears, the incision should be superficial.
And pause the incision of those parts that are lower to allow the pus to drain into the lower parts.
And treat those accordingly with what is appropriate for their cure.
Chapter XLII.
On the incision of scrofulas that occur in the neck.
These abscesses often occur in the neck, armpits, and groin; sometimes they also appear in other parts of the body.
And those that occur in the neck often develop between the muscles, and every scrofula among them is within its own proper membrane, just as in nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
There are species of these scrofulous growths that are nodules, for only some of them are lithic, and of those only some contain moisture, and of those only the malignant ones which do not respond to treatment.
Therefore, in the one which you find to be well disposed upon examination, of which the color that is manifest is similar to the color of the skin, and it is soft throughout, and it is not adhered to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and it does not hurt, so you should incise it at a dry superficial part above the upper part of the body from line G down to line B, and scrape it out entirely, and extend two flaps of the skin an inch or inches if you need that, as we have said concerning the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is more cautious that the vein or nerve not be incised.
And let the incision not be sharp, so that it does not cause you to use your hand in the incision or disturb the patient, and do not incise what is unnecessary; for if you should incise a vein or artery and prohibit yourself from further operation, then pause with a vitriol powder or other powders which induce bleeding, and apply a bandage to the wound, and leave that inside where the acuteness of the abscess subsides and the inflammation is mollified and begins to form pus. When the bleeding has stopped.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then inquire at the time of the operation if there is any cause for other small scrofulous growths, and then incise those and clean them.
And if there is a large vein in the root of the scrofula, avoid incising that part of its root. And be careful to bandage that area firmly with linen and leave it so that no harm comes to it.
And then fill the wound with old cotton that you have already soaked in Egyptian oil, and then treat it.
And if you incise the entire scrofula, then be sure to approximate the two lips of the wound and sew them together immediately so that there is no room for any superfluity.
But if you see that there is room for superfluous fluid due to the size of the scrofula, then be sure to incise its root, and drain it and administer sutures as has been said.
And for those scrofulas that contain humidity, make a superficial puncture where the place of maturation appears to you, and proceed with a puncture to allow the pus to drain from deep within, as we have said.
And then proceed with punctures, apply ointment and treatments suitable for such cases, so that it corrodes what is necessary for the corruption. And when you see the wound already cleansed, then treat it with an ointment of flesh-generating properties until it is healed.
Chapter XLIII.
On the incision of the pustule of an accidental abscess in the groin.
Thus did the ancients call this section in the epitome, and no one has seen such an operation in our land.
And this is the inscription of the speech of those people.
In men who have a weak constitution, it is advisable to avoid the extraction of the epilhot, as doing so would not be beneficial because all the veins and major channels are here.
In those who have boils or abscesses in the mouth, throat, or ears, when the illness is not in the bone, it is advisable to use the section of the epilhot to avoid the loss that would be caused by perforation.
Therefore, when we want to do this, the epilhot should be incised below three circles of its own circles, or four if it is a smaller section along the latitude in the place between two circles by quantity. The section should be made in ciphac, not in cartilage, and this location is suitable for sectioning because there is little flesh and the blood vessels are large.
And if the physician is cautious, it is advisable to first stretch the throat skin for an inch and then make a cut until reaching the canal, but avoid the blood vessels if you see any reason to do so.
And then incise the ciphac as we have recommended; this is significant for the section of the canal by the membrane which is attached to that part and by the separation of the voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Thus when the hour arrives in which the perforation is feared, join two lips of the wound, and cut away that one alone with the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that cause flesh to grow until it is healed.
And Al-Bucasis says that the essence of what we have recommended is to observe that some of these abscesses have already obstructed the windpipe of the patient, and the patient has come close to death, and their breath has been cut off, and some people incise the epiglottis so that the patient can breathe over the site of the wound by respiration, and thereby be saved from death.
And for this reason, it is commanded to leave the wound open until the malice of the disease is consumed; and the malice lasts for three days; therefore, during that time, treat the wound until it is healed.
And what I have seen with my own eyes is that a servant took a knife and inserted it into their windpipe and cut out a part of the trachea, and I was called to treat them, and I heard the moaning as if it were the groan of the dying, and uncovered the wound and saw the blood that had come from the wound.
Thus I admitted that she had not cut any vein or artery, and air was coming out of the wound, and I treated her until she was healed.
And nothing happened to the servant except that her voice became hoarse, and after some days she returned to the use of her usual condition.
Here I say that in the section of the episiotomy there is no fear.
Chapter XLIV.
Of the section on abscess which occurs in the groin outside, and is called hernia of the groin.
This abscess called hernia of the groin is a large abscess according to the color of the skin, and it occurs in women.
And it happens in two ways, for it is either natural or accidental.
And in the natural case, there is no need for treatment.
And the accidental one happens in two ways, one of which resembles fatty knots, and the other way resembles an abscess caused by arterial obstruction, and in that case there is fear. And it does not help to apply heat except in the case where it is small. And you should avoid that and investigate it as described, and if you find it resembling fatty knots and not attached to any veins, then incise it as you would with knots and extract it with water mixed with oil and bursiclam; and if not, thoroughly examine its nature, and subsequently treat the area with what is necessary for treatment until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.
Of the section on types of knots.
There are various types of knots, and I have already mentioned all their types in the division.
And so I recommend that you distinguish between a knot and an exit wound where there is doubt.
Therefore, I say that in an exit wound there is heat and fever and severe pain until the effervescence of the excess stops; and complete putrefaction occurs when at that time the fever, the pain, and the heat cease.
But in a knot there is no heat nor fever nor pain, and it resembles that which happens with the lion's paw which is in its own skin.
And it depends on the color of the skin, and its beginning is like a vesicle and it forms in such a way, whether larger or smaller, and they are of two types: dry or fatty, and contain moisture, and the colors of moisture are mottled, as I say in the division.
Therefore, when you arrive at the treatment of a knot, you should express that and inquire about it at the beginning of the chapter which is called introduction, as its form will come in the chapter which is near this one.
And this is the method of inquiry for abscesses and all knots, which is that you take this instrument and insert it into the most humid part which you find in the abscess, and you will revolve it a little at a time until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the tissue.
And then withdraw it gradually according to the measure of the size of the abscess, and then extract the instrument and examine what has come out in its wake.
And if the moisture flows out, of whatever color it may be, then you shall incise it at a shallow section as I have said regarding other abscesses.
And if there is no moisture from the penetration, then you should know that it is dry.
And if it is dry around the wound in the shape of a cross according to this form: As you have been taught, suspend the dressing over the wound, and carefully remove the crust from all sides, and preserve the hair or skin if possible over it, and extract that pus at the nodes.
But if it is torn during the operation, it is not possible to extract that pus, and most times in such cases, you will extract it gradually so that none of it remains due to any cause.
For if any of it remains due to some cause, little or much, the abscess will return according to the severity.
And if you see that some of it remains in a small part, then cover the wound with some caustic powder and harden the wound, and place over it something suitable for an abscess, and treat that from another method until it is healed.
And if it is a large abscess, then you should cut both sides of it and treat that with what promotes healing.
But if a pulsating or non-pulsating vein is opposite it, then you should cauterize and fill the area with three applications of vitriol, letting that remain open for two or three days until the wound heals and the blood stops boiling; and then return to incising what was torn from the abscess.

These abscesses that come from lymph nodes are among the types of scrofula, hard lumps covered with skin that can be moved.

Chapter XLIX.
On the Incision of Abscesses that Occur Due to Artery or Vein.
When an artery is ruptured and the wound has healed over it, an abscess may occur. The signs by which it can be known whether inflammation and abscess are due to an artery or a vein: for when the abscess is due to an artery, it tends to aggregate in the upper part of the body; and if you press on the abscess with your fingers, you will hear a hissing sound.
And that which is from a vein is round at the surface of the body.
And surgery on this abscess is an error, especially in those areas near the armpits, and in the angles, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; for it is very dangerous, therefore it should be avoided that its treatment with a knife be eschewed, and those that are in the extremities or on the head, it should be avoided.
But for that which is due to inflammation of the artery opening, make an incision in the appropriate section along its length; and near the place where you will cut with hooks near the artery, and dissect the tunics until it is exposed.
And then place on that needle and make it penetrate into the other side, and secure the artery with a double stitch of fine thread, — which procedure I explain in the extraction of two arteries which are found in the thighs.
And then apply to the place which is between two ligaments with a spatula so that the blood flows out completely while it is there, and the abscess be dissolved.
And then administer treatment in which pus is drawn out so that the ligaments can fall; and then treat it with appropriate remedies so that it may be healed.
And if it is an abscess due to a vein incision, now you should have in your hand what is possible regarding the abscess at that place, and then insert the needle below the place where you had placed your hand, and make it penetrate; and tie this thread doubly so that you can extract it from the other side, and then ligature the abscess with a strong ligament as I have told you about the grapevine ligature on both sides of the thread's extremities.
If you fear that you will extract the thread, then now insert a needle under the abscess; first place the point of the needle, and strengthen its points in four places.
Approach the abscess in its middle part when it is full inside so that when what is inside comes out, then you cut open the excess of the skin, and leave what is attached to it.
And then apply to the wound the powder which you have soaked in wine and oil, and then administer the treatment made with cauterization and herbs until it is healed.
Chapter I of the abscess which occurs due to the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess can occur in arteries and veins, and similarly, an abscess occurs in the nerves when there is a blow to them or due to excessive work and similar causes.
And it is done with the hand in the places of the assistants or at the heel, or in any place where the joints are usually troubled.
And it is a severe abscess, and its color resembles that of the rest of the body, and it is among the worst conditions when it is constricted by violence, and when it affects the patient, they seem to be in a stupor.
And it is not an abscess that forms at the front of the body, and for this reason it is below the skin, and it affects all parts equally and does not spread forward or backward.
Therefore, when it is in the joints, there is no need for it to be encased in iron, as it will easily lead to contraction.
Cut in what is on the head or forehead with a spatum (a type of knife). If it is a small abscess, cut that out with forceps and remove it by roots.
And if it is large, then suspend it with incisions, and expose that, and remove that subsequently; and stitch the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
About the incision of an abscess that occurs on the lip as it would occur on the abdomen.
Indeed, in some people there appear swellings on their abdomens and other parts of their bodies, which are called fungous because they resemble mushrooms, the cause of which is subtle but the appearance is large; and often the edges of these are smooth, and it can be small in size, yet magnificent in its nature.

Chapter LIIB.
On the Treatment of a Swelling at the Navel.
A swelling of the navel is caused by various reasons; it may be due to fissures in the abdomen that are above the stomach, and for this reason, a patch made of cloth or intestine is applied as with other ruptures; or it can be from a wound where blood flows from a vein or artery, depending on the circumstances, or it may be caused by trapped air.
But if it is due to a fissure in the abdomen and the appearance of a cloth patch, then the color of the swelling is similar to the color of the skin; it is painless, and soon appears in various situations. And if it is due to the exit of the bowels, then the situation is different from what we have described, with much greater diversity, and when you press that area with your fingers, it feels hidden, and immediately returns; and it is very light in there, and is magnified by the introduction of bath and vigorous labor.
And if it is due to humidity, then it is loose and not hidden when you press with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor is it hard.
And if it is due to blood, then with these signs an abscess appears turning black.
And if it is due to flesh that has rotted, then the abscess is severe, large and hard, and is seated according to a quantity.
And if it is due to gas, then the touch of it is soft.
And the operation in this case is that you must consider whether the prominence of the navel is due to blood from an artery, or a vein, or gas; now it is advisable for you to be cautious about treating it, because there is fear and dread as I have taught you in the chapter where I have discussed abscesses from arteries and veins.
And if the prominence of the navel is due to the bowels or the membrane, then it is advisable for you to order the patient to hold their breath and stand extended; then feel around the entire circumference of the navel, and then order them to lie on their back with both hands under their buttocks; then surround the area with a strip of linen in the circumference of the navel over the place where you have felt with your hand; then extend half of the abscess upwards for about an inch; then tie it around the circumference tightly or loosely according to a proper method.
From the side where the abscess is spread over the membrane, insert your finger into that area and search for the bag; and if you have found or secured it with the membrane, then extract it immediately and push the bag to the inner parts of the abdomen.
And if you have found the membrane, then take that one inch and incise the excess tissue from it.
But if it is contrary to your artery or vein, then encircle that spot, and return to your operation; take two needles and place them in those two strong points and insert two needles around the circle you made around the abscess in a cross shape, which you should make penetrate; and then divide the abscess into four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area so that the extended flesh can drain and fall off by itself; or incise it when it becomes thinner and will drain, and then treat it with water from a suitable liquid until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the navel is of flesh or due to moisture, then you should proceed to conquer the abscess as we have said and extract the blood or moisture that you find there, and then treat it with a suitable liquid that will heal and consolidate the area.
Chapter LIII.
On the Treatment of Cancers.
We have already discussed in the division, the types of cancer and how to approach its treatment with medicines, and that it is cautious in treating it with iron so that it does not become ulcerated; we have also mentioned cancer generated in the breast and the care in its treatment.
And let the ancients say that in some cases cancer is in a place where it is possible to eradicate it, just as when it occurs in the breast or on the nipple and similar parts assigned for extracting all of it, especially when it begins small.
But when it is old and hard, do not try to deal with it yourself, for I cannot heal anyone from that nor have I seen another who could reach such a state.
And the operation on it is when it is assigned, as we have said, that you precede and leave the patient and purge from black bile for some days, and then bleed them if there is obvious congestion.
And then prepare the patient for the preparation in which the operation is located, and then place hooks around those that are needed for it; and then hollow out completely with a well-sharpened curved knife for thorough excision so that no cause of recurrence remains. And let the blood flow freely, and do not bind it too tightly, and press the areas, and clean the thick blood with your hand or with an instrument that is suitable among the available tools.
But if it is contrary to you to have thick blood coagulate in a vein or artery, then cauterize that area until the flow of blood stops, and afterward treat it with another method until it heals.

Chapter Sixty-Five.
