On incision for the disease nafir.
This illness is called nafir in our homeland, and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs, and it often changes from one limb to another.
And now I will describe that illness as I will recount it to you. I was called to see a sick woman in a village, and when I uncovered her arm, I found a small inflammation in the funis vein of the arm. After it had been ruptured for about an hour, I saw that this inflammation was spreading as quickly as wax melts and spreads, though more rapidly than would be expected, like living silver flowing from place to place.
And then the pain began in that area and spread into the muscle, and it remained there for about an hour.
And then it spread to the other parts of the body until it reached the other arm.
And then she told me that the illness was spreading throughout her body as I had observed, and I was greatly amazed by its transformation and the speed with which it moved from one limb to another. And I had seen this before in my own practice in this manner, but I have never seen it change so quickly from one limb to another as I did in this case. But I have not seen it happen in my patients as it did in the woman.
And I thought that this was due to the fact that the woman was of delicate constitution, with weak limbs and exposed veins, in which this inflammation appeared as it changed.
It is necessary that this counter-measure not appear in those who are delicate and of weak corporeal vein blood.
And when you want to treat it, and the patient feels that pain, at that moment do not appear as we have said, but then strengthen around it and gently press on it until that trapped air is released, and cauterize the area.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then treat that by evacuating the body and expelling air, and use potions to dissolve foul pills and similar remedies.
Of the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are varied according to their types, and according to the parts of the body where they strike, for their diversity is that some have large points, and others small, concave, blunt, or solid; and among them are those with three angles, and those with tongues; and there are also those which are barbed.
Of the signs of the head are when it falls onto the arrow, and it passes through the bone, and exits from the sphenoid which is above the brain, for it happens due to violent soda, and scotoma, and vertigo, and hardness in the eyes, and inflammation and hardness of the tongue, and spasm and mixture of understanding and vomiting of bile, and easily is severed speech and destroyed voice, and exits from the place of the wound humidity white like dust, and there appears on the face moisture of the flesh.
And so if you perceive these signs in me, then you should abstain from treatment and the extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart, so that it is near the left nipple, and feels as if it were lodged in a hard place, not a soft one; and easily the movement of the arrow resembles the pulse, and blood from the wound is black and thick, and coldness affects the extremities, and cold sweat appears; therefore you know that death will come very soon.
And the signs when the arrow has pierced the lung are that foam-like blood comes from the wound, and the veins near the neck become congested, and the patient's color changes, and he exhales a great breath, and seeks to draw in cold air.
And if the arrow falls on the membrane which is in the buttocks, then the pain at that moment is near the small ribs, and it causes great and violent pain and sighing, and one cannot move any of the body's muscles.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then very soon there appears from the wound some undigested food.
And if the arrow is stuck in the belly, and it is embedded within, make some sort of incision into the wound, or the cloth, or gut which is ruptured, and there is no hope for the cure without the extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine comes out with it, and the process causes some harm to the parts outside, and there is severe pain over the wound, then you know that it is fatal.
But the other members as well, such as the face, the neck, and the jaw, the shoulders and the helpers, and the vertebrae of the back, and the collarbone, and the hip and similar parts of the limbs, are saved according to the best dispositions when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery and is not a poisonous arrow.
And I recount this to you as I have seen regarding the nature of these arrows, so that it may signify to you about your own treatment.

Of the signs of a bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is known: its twisting, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and how it feels when you press upon it with your hand.

Chapter Second: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct. Some fractures are caused by the impact of an object, which removes all the bone within its circumference, leaving only what is outside this area, just as it would in wood; thus, this type is called a crush. Or an object may cut some part of the bone but does not go through to the other side and cuts into the flesh without penetrating further; this type of fracture is called absolute elevation.
And the severity of these two fractures can be either great or slight, and there is also a fracture that is comminuted or crushed; the cause of this is impact with something weak or falling on a stone, among similar circumstances.
And this fracture is either penetrating that approaches the peritoneum which is below the bone; or it is in the face of the bone, and the harm of these two fractures is great or small.
And of the fracture which is inflicted on the bone, in the subtlety of the thin strands, and by this it is named this particular type of fracture capillary.
And it is a fracture that is made either by falling or by the impact of a stone and similar objects, which causes some plate of bone to enter into its parts, and it is made in the place of concavity just as happens with the bones of the arm when they occur due to their impact and are broken upon entering the inner parts.
And this is mostly made in the bones of human bodies, just as it happens with the heads of children.
And for all these types of fractures, treatments are used, which are pieces just like small plates separated. And I will return to the recall of all this in its place.
And you should know all these types of fractures by detection on them and by inquiry into their attempts and the remnants of corrupted flesh that are upon them.
And the capillary type of fracture is known by this: it is revealed on the bone, and it is wiped clean, and lined upon it with a probe, because the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider at the beginning the symptoms of the patient, and if you see by these symptoms that it signifies by clear indication extreme fear, such as vomiting like bile, stretching out, loss of understanding, hoarseness of voice, fainting, acute fever, and similar conditions with their rigor, and similar to them among the major dispositions, then suspect that they will not be healed.
And if for uncommon accidents you hope for health, then you begin the treatment of them.

And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you excise the bone with sharp scissors, and this is the form of it: And subsequently administer another excisor slightly wider than the previous one; and this is the form of it:
Then apply the knife to cut out the pus that is there.
And it is necessary for you to have various knives, some of which are used to cut out pus deeper than others; and some are sharper at their tips than the others, and they are made of iron or steel.
And use them with ease in tapping on the knives so that you do not blunt the tip and edge of them.
And if the bone is hard and strong, then it is necessary to drill around it before you apply the knives, using the instruments called non-penetrating chisels.
And they are not called this only because these do not reach the end of the bone from where they start, for the chisel has a rounded tip or margin above which is the sharp point resembling a border or small circle, which prevents it from penetrating beyond the thickness of the bone.
It is convenient for you to have many of these chisels, each of which should match the thickness of each bone, so that at your disposal for the entire skull, the tip of which is sharp, and the length and width are proportionate to the thickness of this part.
This is the method for three types of chisels: The quality of drilling around the broken bone is that you place the chisel on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then move the chisel to another location, and continue this process throughout the entire thickness of the bone or as much as needed.
And then pour in the instrument with water that is between each fragment of bone, and apply the poultice as finely as you can on top of it for levity, as we have said, so that you remove the bone, or with your hand, or by some other means through which you will have prepared for this, such as a sponge and fine forceps.
And make sure that you guard with all caution that the instrument does not touch any part of the wound.
And so when you have circumscribed the bone to be perforated, and it will be separated from the wound if it is continuous there, and you will extract it, make sure that you scrape and smooth its roughness with something like an instrument but which is finer and sharper than other instruments.
And if any small and fragmented parts of the bones remain and you are having difficulty with them, now take something with ease from what is available to you among the instruments; and then treat the wound with lint and the medicines that we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is light, distant from fear, which Gualia says and praises greatly; and this is what he says: It is necessary for you at the beginning to start uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and obvious, until when you have uncovered that part and have paused just below it with this slow and cheerful instrument of which this is the form:
And the lens-shaped part is solid and does not require cutting any part of it, while the sharp-pointed part should be divided into two along its length, as you see; that the lens-shaped part fits the wound with its acute face towards the bone.
And then strike with the chisels of a part with a small hammer inside so that you cut all the bone with ease as it is revolved, and you are sure of the membrane that no accident happens in any way, and if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the least knowledgeable among them; certainly, and if he were sleeping.
Now if there is any cause attached to the bone of the membrane in some places of the bone, cut that which has the end of the chisel shaped like a lens, and deliver that which has ease, for it is delivered without injury and fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the membrane, and what is abscissed or remains of the bone is its surface, and it remains rough with small irregularities, now you should scrape that roughness and eradicate those irregularities with subtle razors for which you have prepared in various quantities so that it is possible for you to administer them appropriately at every place where they are most suitable, and the most correct ones according to how your operation on that broken bone and its shape leads you.
And you should administer in your proportion with which you scrape the bone at the beginning with those razors that are the widest, and then use successively those that are more subtle, and do not cease doing this continuously until you reach administering the thinnest and most subtle of all of them.
But in each of the other small capillary fractures and subtle fracture, you should administer care according to what leads you towards the correction of it.
And it is a cause that one does not fear among all those who have studied in that art. Stand on what we have written about large fractures.
And if the abdominal wall is rectangular when you remove the bone, now cover it with a piece of cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse that in warm oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; then take another folded or triple-layered cloth and immerse it in vinegar and warm oil, and place it on top of the first cloth; and do this as quickly as possible without pressing hard, so as not to damage the tissue; then apply a linen bandage over that, but do not tighten it beyond what is necessary for covering the wound.
Leave that in this treatment for one day or two until you are sure there is no abscess; then loosen it and apply medicines that have a surplus of dryness, such as linseed paste and flour poultices, and powders of onions and aristolochia, and similar things.
And it is that you should make these medicines into a powder that is finely ground and applied to the wound, as they are dry; generally, apply any medicine whose property is to cleanse without irritating.
And guard yourself according to your virtue that the wound in your treatment remains clean; and it should not be in the negligence of handling or any other carelessness; nor should you allow its virulence to increase. For when it is properly set on the abdominal wall, it corrupts that area and weakens it, and thus leads to severe illness due to this malpractice.
And it affects the tissue of the head, as when the bone is cleaned and especially when the treatment of it is delayed, there appears a black discoloration on its surface; and therefore consider now whether these accidents lead to illness, and know that it is harmful with all certainty.
And if the darkness does not come from the medicine that is applied above it but rather from the virtue of that medicine which causes that darkness, then you should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, and mix these together; then place a cloth soaked in this mixture over the wound; and afterwards treat it with the spices used for healing until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is broken in only two parts above mostly, or one of those sometimes, because there are two bones there, since the part below is cartilaginous and does not break; what happens to it is bruising, swelling, and distortion.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should insert your finger into the side of the nose, and adjust that fracture with your fingertips inside and outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful that your operation does not cause pain to the patient.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then you should use the handle end of a probe with some thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you should proceed as described.
And when it comes to the restoration of it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; otherwise, you should wait until the seventh or tenth day for the abscess to be cauterized.
Insert a piece of cloth into the end of the nose, if the fracture is in one piece, or two pieces of cloth, if the fracture is in two pieces, and let the piece of cloth be large enough to fill the trough of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the cloths should be dipped in boiled honey and changed every day, but I have not seen this myself; however, it is good to dip the cloth in albumen from egg combined with powdered sugar; then leave the cloth inside until the bone is firm and the cartilage has hardened.
And sometimes a piece of wax is inserted into the nose instead of cloth, wrapped in lion's skin cloth; and this should be done when the fracture of the nose is more severe and the patient is not prohibited from breathing through it.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you want, do it this way, and if you want, use cloths.
And if another swelling occurs that needs to be cauterized, apply a plaster to the nose made of wax or cloth dipped in vinegar and rose oil, or with frankincense.
But if it does not require cauterization, now you should apply a plaster outside made of sesame flour and powdered coriander mixed with egg white; then place this on a piece of lion's skin, and do not cover the nose with anything else.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or comminuted, now you should dissect around them, and extract them with instruments suitable for that; then close the gap, and treat it with substances that promote healing and consolidation, and with appropriate plasters.
And if it happens that a swelling occurs inside the nose, now you must treat it with cold applications and administer lead tubes within to ensure complete healing.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated.
When the lower jaw is broken and there is no swelling around the fracture, check now if the fracture is only on the surface and not split into two parts and curved inward; in this case, its diagnosis is easy.
And you should, if the fracture is on the right side, insert your left thumb into the mouth of the patient; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jawbone, then insert your right thumb; and push the bone fragments inward with ease using your other hand from the outside of the bone; do this gently.
And if the jaw fracture is already separated into two parts, then you should administer extension of both parts according to straightness as much as possible until they are aligned.
And if you encounter a comminuted fracture of the teeth and dislocation, then strengthen those that you expect will break with a wire of gold or silver or silk; and immediately apply cold to the fractured jaw, and then apply a doubled cloth, and place over it a properly made bandage or a piece of soft material equal in length to the jaw; and immediately bind it above as you have prepared for the binding and as is convenient for the application, so that it is not destroyed.
And he commands the patient to use rest and calmness, and to pause from liquid food.
And if you find that the shape has been altered in any way, then on the third day you should examine and loosen it; and afterward correct what has already been altered with moist flour, egg white, or chickpea flour, after you have removed the cerate from it, and place wool soaked in it over the plaster.
And then when you have attached and adjusted that plaster to it, and the position of the bone has not been altered, leave it, do not loosen it until it is healed and the fracture is strengthened; for in many cases this fracture becomes strong in three weeks.
And if in other cases an abscess occurs, then administer what has been said many times about the treatment of it until it is healed, and drain that abscess.
And if the fracture is with a splinter, then consider whether it is a piece of bone separated or a splinter, and carefully extract those splinters with what is appropriate for removing them using the instruments.
And if the opening of the splinter is narrow, then enlarge it with a scalpel according to the quantity necessary for your needs.
And then when you have removed those splinters, and if any cannot be drained, close the opening of the splinter if it is wide; and if not, place one of the plasters that are appropriate for it on it to promote healing until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
On the restoration of the fork when it is fractured.
Most often the fork is broken in the part before the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
For it is broken, or separated, or separated into two parts such that no displacement occurs; and it is easy for its restoration. Or it is such that this happens with displacement, and its restoration is difficult. Or it is such that the fracture involves an injury.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture involves an injury, is that you make two assistants present, so that one holds the assistant instrument and supports the broken fork, and the other extends the collar around that part.
And then mold the fracture with the tools mentioned so that its shape is made as needed, and there should be neither a small prominence nor a depression.
And if you need more extension, then place underneath the tillicum of a malaute sheet or wool, and its size should be according to your necessity; and extend and elevate the fork and compress the pad with your hand so that you mold the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot draw the extremity of the fork to the outer parts, so that it reaches the preon, then the patient should be reclined on their neck, and place something underneath the muscle about half as large in size.
And the assistant presses the muscle at the sides so that the bone of the fork is elevated to reach the preon, and then you correct the fracture and mold it with the tools mentioned.
If you feel pain from the forked part and its knobs, now you must cut into that area and extract those splinters easily.
And if it is stuck in the bone, now you must devise an incision with one of the instruments that you have prepared for this purpose, since you had paused below the forked part with the tool that serves as the key; and this tool is made of wood or iron, and its form is as follows:
And it is similar to the hollow part of a bowl, whose width should be according to what you need from it, depending on the size of the bone or the space available.
But the length of it should be as much as is possible for the operation; it should have two ends, one end wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound you have incised when extracting the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are sure it will form an abscess, now you should bring together the two lips of the wound with stitches.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear it may form an abscess, now you should cover the wound with patches and powders according to the quantity of the wound's incision.
And if an abscess does occur, now apply a patch made of soft cloth and rose oil mixed with vinegar, and place it over the area.
And do not let the splint be loose above the broken bone; and so it is necessary that you see the patient every day, and when the bandage has softened or you see the splint already loosened, then you must correct this and tighten the bandaging.
And place the patient's pillow on his neck, and put down below him a small cushion beneath his neck so that during sleep his neck is slightly raised; thus raise the support from his back, and therefore elevate the fracture of the collarbone to the elevation of the muscle; or bind his arm to his neck.
And do not remove the bandage unless it is necessary due to an abscess in the area, within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see his face has changed due to this, and adjust it in the constriction and restore it until it is set and the fracture of the collarbone is healed.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the method in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some people this is done in less time.
Chapter VI.
On the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is broken in the middle and not broken into pieces but only at the edges.
And when it is broken in that place — and it is broken in the middle of it — and it is known only by touch, then according to the figure of the fracture prepare yourself and reduce it according to its natural shape in every way that is possible for you.
And then apply powdered alum mixed with honey and stop the lion; and then apply powdered wool doubled over; and then sprinkle on the table a fine layer of powder according to the quantity of the spoon, either all or just a little.
And if you leave the layer concave in some places of the spoon, then you fill those concavities with lint, and then level the layer according to equality.
And then press from above with a humid cloth of appropriate length and with firmness on the layer so that it is not moved from its place.
And examine the dressing each day; and every time it is softened, change it on another day and press it, and level the layer if it has moved from its place.
And place the dressing over the wound as appropriate.
And the spoon is restored in 20 days or in 25 days.
And when this number of days is completed, then you remove the dressing, and be sure that these are bones for which the beginning was not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the place of the broken bone and place a bandage there, then you should cut above it, remove it, and act as we have said regarding the fracture of the shoulder blade or in case of suppuration or cauterization if such occurs.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of a foot fracture.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle and remains close together; however, its extremities move apart as cicatrization and comminution occur.
Of the accidents which occur when the bone is broken are that it inclines towards the parts inside and causes great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, fever, and vomiting of blood; and it is apparent in the cavity in the broken bone and is not hidden from sensation.
And the restoration of it is that the patient be laid on their back, and a bolster placed between the shoulders; and then press the muscles, and adjust the ribs with both hands, and delicately perform the operation and adjustment in every possible way suitable for this purpose, until the figure of the bone returns as required.
And then place on the plaster and cotton, and place below thin strips of wood or reeds or similar lightweight materials, as you wrap them; and then carefully bind that over the broken bone without pressing too hard; and proceed with bandaging around the back in several layers, and tighten that binding appropriately; and then check the bandage at all times, and when it has softened, tighten that again.
And if necessity requires the removal of that bandage due to corrosion or swelling, or pain, or an abscess that has occurred in the area, then you should remove it and loosen the plaster and correct what has happened according to the method of treatment; and then reapply the plaster, if needed for this purpose; and adjust the binding until it is healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of the ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in thick areas where they meet at the sides; and from their extremities in front, those do not suffer contusion but rather because they are cartilaginous. And concerning the nerve, there is no deception as to its sensation; you must investigate it carefully as mentioned.
And the restoration should be such that it equalizes the fracture as mentioned by the possible method, within which it should be aligned as necessary, and then apply a plaster to strengthen the broken bone if you find it necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs inclines towards the inner parts, then it causes severe and piercing pain, like the piercing that occurs when one is weeping, and because of this, the bone places a membrane over itself; and there is difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And the Ancients were skilled in that with living things.
And what they say is: it is appropriate to give food to the patient that generates inflammation and flatulence, so that the belly becomes swollen and extended, and thus the fracture is pushed outward.
But we should do this with the condition that it does not lightly cause a dangerous abscess if one does not occur; and if it does occur, then it helps to address that.
And some of them say that it should be applied above the flatulent area and then sucked out with force; this reasoning seems reasonable, but it is feared that the flatulence might draw the superfluities to the site due to weakness.
And some of them say: it is appropriate to cover the area with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and place poultices between the ribs until it is full, making the bandage equal when wrapping it according to its roundness; then treat the patient as you would for pleurisy with food and medicine.
And if the patient is strongly compelled by reason of illness which he cannot endure, and the bone has penetrated through the dressing with a puncture wound, we fear for the patient; at that time it is necessary to cut above the location, expose the broken part, and immediately use the instrument mentioned previously to preserve the joint, and then cut away the bone with ease, and extract it.
And then adjust the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with stitching, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs internally, then you should incise it and apply powder in ointment and place it on the area, and treat the patient with what is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And let him lie supine on the side on which he sleeps until he is healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fractures of the spine of the back and neck.
In some cases, there is a fracture of the neck bones, and it takes time for their healing, similarly with the spine of the back. And when such an accident happens to someone and you want to know if they will heal or not, then consider this: if you see their fingers already softened and numb and dead, and they cannot move those nor extend them nor strain them, and when you press on them with your fingers or pierce them with a needle and no pain is felt in them, then you know that it has not healed, and in such conditions it is mortal.
And if they move those and feel constriction with the fingers and pain from the piercing, then you know that the bone of the neck is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see the vertebrae of the neck appear and you want to know whether it will heal or not, then look at the condition of them.
Now if you see that someone has become weak, and they experience what often happens to those who are ill, and soon when they lie on their back, they vomit and have diarrhea without their will, and sometimes they are forced onto their back and want to urinate but cannot, now you know that it is fatal, and then do not labor in the cure of them.
But if none of those causes occur, the condition is better and more hopeful.
And the treatment for them is to apply a poultice to the abscess until you place over the protruding vertebra olive oil mixed with rose water, or two eggs beaten; apply this several times during the day until the abscess is subdued.
And now apply one of the comforting and restorative plasters, and bandage it over that area; and instruct the patient to use rest and tranquility and not to sleep on the side where they feel pain until it is healed.
And if there is an avulsion of the bone or some cause that has separated it, now you should expose the wound and remove that bone; and then approximate the two edges of the wound, with sutures if it is large; and then treat it with one of the healing ointments until it is healed.
But if the end of the bone is broken at the joint, and it is the end of the cortex, now you should insert your fingers into the buttocks with the left hand, and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by second-degree reduction; and then apply a plaster and splint to it, if you need those, and then bandage it.
And if you feel splinters broken in it, then lay your hand upon that one, remove it, and treat the wound with the treatment which is mentioned beforehand until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the broad area (This could be a reference to a specific part of the body or a particular context in the text; "ancla" might be a misspelling of "anca," which means "flank" or "hip" in some contexts). For clarity, it's often best to provide more context around medical terms.
The bones of the flanks when they are broken, and if they break, then their fracture is nothing but communication at the extremities of those and they are split along their length and incline to the sides; and it happens that the injured person feels pain in that place and puncture, and the limb is stunned when there is a fracture.
And the restoration of it is that you place your hand upon that until it is set in the figure of the fracture.
And if the fracture is at the extremity of it alone, then you bandage that according to what suits you by equal binding until it is aligned with its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the long bone or one that has already inclined to the sides, then you make the injured person lie on their back until you are prepared for the restoration of that fracture. And when you have bandaged that one, apply a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or cane upon it, and bind it tightly so that there is no fear of puncture of the fracture or movement of the splint.
And bandage the cavity with what fills it until it takes the binding equally, and command the injured person to sleep on their back or side.
If an inflamed abscess is cauterized, at that moment you must stop its extension and its restoration until the cauterized abscess is calmed, and pause on it with what precedes; then return to its restoration, and strengthen it as needed.
And if you encounter them in the bone or they are crushed, then treat them at that moment.
Chapter XI.
On the restoration of a fracture of the wrist.
The adjutori is what is between the forearm and the top of the shoulder.
And if it is fractured, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, one of which is that you take a flat splint of moderate thickness in this form:
And let them be held at both ends with two bandages, and then it should be suspended from a high place.
And have the patient lie on a mattress; then place the fractured arm over the splint until it reaches its midpoint, where the curvature of the splint is attached.
And then place something heavy on it, or have the patient extend that arm downward; and then the physician should keep the fracture in its original position until the fracture returns as needed.
And the other way is that the patient lies supine on their neck and suspends the arm at the neck with bandages; then command two assistants, one of whom holds what is above the fracture by its sides; and the other holds what is below it; each extending in their own direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then tie one strip of cloth around the fracture and another around it, and each of the two assistants should pull on their respective ends.
And if the fracture is close to the muscle's extremity, then you should place half of the cloth strip over the tendon and the other half below the fracture, pulling it taut.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the joint, then you should place the cloth strip over that location and around the joint.
And similarly, tie it with ease and comfort, and as soon as it is adjusted properly and applied well, then tighten it if no abscess occurs.
But if an abscess does occur, then loosen the binding within seven days, and place over it a piece of wool soaked in vinegar and rose water until the abscess subsides, and then tighten it again.
And the method of binding it is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then wrap a bandage made of cloth around the plaster; then adjust the arm with the aid of this setup, and place your hand on their muscle.
And place the cloth and bind around the support so that the arm is in a fixed position, unless some cause related to the broken bone prohibits this.
And now use splints.
And place on the fracture that splint which is thicker and stronger than the others; place between each splint a width equal to the width of a finger, and make the length of the splints according to the break, adding three finger widths from both ends.
And place strips over the stitches which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandaging over the site of the fracture be made tighter. And the farther the fracture is apart, the bandaging should be less tight.
And thus, when you find the position of the splints and the bandaging as we have said during the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then do so.
And if you fear an abscess may form, then you should loosen the bandaging and splints by the seventh day as we have said.
And therefore, consider the bandaging in all parts for three days so that no swelling or inflammation occurs at the site; or it is prohibited for food to reach the limb due to excess tightness of the bandaging; and thus correct all of this as we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause of this will occur, then do not loosen the bandaging until after three days or longer.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their arms be positioned over their stomach.
And place a pad filled with wool over the assistant, and at all times of the night or day ensure that the figure of the broken limb is not loose or dissolved, or that the bandaging is too slack; therefore, correct this as needed with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we mentioned earlier, such that the food is light at first until bandaging begins; and then gradually increase the richness of the diet.
As to the usual course of treatment and the care of the wound, it is that they be kept clean for forty days; and at this time it must be loosened, bathed, and treated with medicines which correct it.
And if the fracture is comminuted, then you do not loosen it from that bandage and the splints for one day or even two months.
Chapter XII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the ulna and radius; of which one is small, and it is that which lies next to the wrist, and the other is larger and is situated below the small one, towards the lower part.
And if the ulna is broken alone, or the smaller one, and often both together.
And when the smaller ulna is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is quicker.
And when the ulna is broken below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller ulna above, then it is appropriate for the physician, during its restoration, to allow a small extension with ease until it is bandaged.
But if the larger ulna is the one that is broken, then it is necessary to allow more vigorous extension.
And if you have cut both falces together, then it is necessary that you press the incision more strongly.
And it is necessary that you place the figure of the hand for the restoration of its function and the extension of it lying on a cushion, with the wrist above all parts, so that all the other fingers are spread out, and the thumb be lower than the others, and the patient lie reclining on a tilted bed.
And let there be a cushion in front of him at an appropriate height or straight ahead, so that the patient does not have to strain.
And let one assistant extend the arm from below with the patient's hand or with a bandage, and another assistant extend it similarly from above.
And let the physician then place the bone inside so that it reduces it into the best position possible.
And if in the process of setting the bone you encounter difficulty, then it is necessary to reduce all parts to their proper place with your skill.
And if an obstacle appears separately that prevents the reduction and is not within your hope for restoration, then you must act on it and remove that according to the method we have described above.
But if the fracture is due to inflammation, I have already treated this in a separate chapter, so follow the treatment described there.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you should line a cloth with cerot made of olive oil and white wax, which is midway between thickness and subtlety, and place a strong cloth over it with gentle pressure so that the abscess can subside; then remove the cerot and apply a plaster prepared from powdered herbs mixed with egg whites, and afterwards place bandages; let the bandage placed over that fracture be wider and stronger.
And know that the number of stars on the arm is seen in major dispositions, or rather the fracture in one bone or in two bones.
And apply pressure firmly and vigorously at the site of the fracture, and when you begin to loosen it either above or below the fracture, apply a little less force according to how it was described at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that you wrap around the fracture should be soft and moistened, not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is strengthened should be of a thickness between fine and coarse, as we have recommended.
And consider the limb and the bandaging after some days. And if any cause arises for which correction is necessary, such as inflammation that occurs in the limb, then you should immerse the limb in hot water until the inflammation subsides.
And allow the limb to rest without constraint until it is at ease, and then reapply the bandaging.
And if the bandaging has already been loosened and the bone is already displaced as described, then you should correct all of this with your skill.
And carefully consider if the nutrition is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive bandaging, for then you should loosen it slightly and allow it to rest for some days until nutrition reaches it, and then strengthen it.
And if the patient does not experience any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to remove the bandaging for about twenty days or thereabouts.
And so the hand of the patient is suspended by its upper part for active movement, and make the patient lie on their side.
And know that this fracture is restored in xxx days or in xxxii days, or it may be restored in xxviii days, and all of this depends on the condition of the tissues and the strength of the patient.
Chapter XIII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the thumb and of the said bones.
Know that to the thumb and to the joints of the said fingers, when they are fractured, and it does not happen to them except with multiple injuries.
And when a fracture or injury occurs to the hand, it is necessary that the patient lie in bed and be placed according to equality, and the hand should be laid out extended.
And then the attendant should extend the broken bones, and the physician should align them so that they are set properly.
And now it is necessary to apply plaster and wool, unless an abscess or burn occurs; then place a piece of wood above according to the size of the area, and you should have wrapped that in linen.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm, then make it appear as a sphere of cloth, and instruct the patient to hold that with their fractured palm; then secure it with cloth.
And the wooden splint should be attached to cloth in such a way that it is raised, so that the splint can be applied to the palm; and secure it as needed.
If the fracture is in the bones above, place splints on top of it and another splint below the part, so that the hand may be open and straight; and then bind it, as if the hand were held between the fingers with a bandage.
And if it happens that there is a fracture in one of the bones of the fingers, then if the finger is small, shape it as necessary and bind it to the palm; and if you wish, place a small straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not injured.
But if the fracture is in other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or in the index, or in the little finger, or in the ring finger, then it should be shaped and bound to that finger; or they should all be bound together in continuity, which is better; or a small splint should be placed over it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your treatment, and also regarding that, the disposition of abscesses and opposing conditions — during your operation — according to what is necessary when any cause of those occurs, as it is repeated in the narrative.
Chapter XIV.
On the treatment of fractures of the collarbone.
The collarbone, which is often fractured, reveals itself by sensation according to how it has shifted in front and above.
And the treatment of it is done in such a way that one bandage is wrapped above the fracture and another below the fracture, and the patient lies prone on their belly and face.
Let the attendant extend the bandage according to its quality when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
If it extends towards the ribs of the chest, now the bandage should be applied to the back at the part of the armpit, so that its extension reaches across them, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture extends towards the back, now the bandage should be applied to the back, and its extension should reach the parts below.
And then the physician should align the bone on both sides and ensure it is reduced according to its natural appearance, and the bone is joined by proper conjunction; now it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs, now you should leave it for several days until the abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandaging should be done as follows: you need to wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, fold it two or three times, and press out any excess; then wrap the chest so that it reaches the wrist and the ribs of the chest.
And insert a long strip of cloth between the chest and the bandage near the back at the lower parts, and let the ends of the cloth come from the most prominent part of both sides, and then roll over the chest and the bandage whatever excess there is of the bandage.
And then apply plasters over the chest at the location of the fracture, and place one strip directly over the bone of the rib.
And fill up the empty space between the bowl and the container with soft cloths, so that the constriction may be congealed.
And then begin the binding tightly from above at the site of the fracture with three or four layers, and as much as you move away from the bandage from the site of the fracture, your binding should be looser and lighter and softer.
And then extend the two ends of the bandage downward to the parts below until it reaches the nail of the foot, and tie both ends of the bandages from the other side so that the binding does not move from its place.
And then place the bandage over it in such a way that corrosion, pus formation, swelling, or similar issues do not occur to the limb; and if any of these causes occur, cut through the bandaging at that point and immediately correct what has happened, as we have said previously.
But if there is a piece of bone that needs to be placed in the bone, then you should try to align it as best as possible; and if not, fix it over it and move it, and treat it according to what precedes recovery until it is healed.
And in some cases, this fracture of the bone is tied with another bandage as we have said regarding the straps and the arm.
But in the restoration of the limb done in such a manner, a bad limp should not occur; and if it is restored alone or with another bandage applied, then it is necessary that the person limps for the rest of their life.
And know that the cord is torn after forty days or a few months, and all of this depends on the diversity of temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter XV.
On the restoration of a fracture of the lower jawbone.
Know that a fracture of the lower jawbone often occurs, but it rarely happens in the front part.
But if such a fracture does occur, it is usually just a crack or bruise in those parts, and this affects one or several areas, and all of this depends on the sensation.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that what is broken in those parts is aligned with the adjacent areas so that it can be joined and united as much as possible by adjustment, ease, and fabrication.
And then apply a plaster and place it over that round bandage if needed; and bind tightly with a bandage as is appropriate.
And then consider all the conditions we have recommended for other fractures, as well as abscesses and similar treatments that oppose any accident in such a way that it is suitable for healing.
Chapter XVI.
On the restoration of a fracture of the chin.
Know that in the chin there are two bones, one of which is thick and is called the chin bone; the other is thin and is called the finial bone.
And it happens with some of the types of fracture that happen to the two bones of the arm, and by this restoration is made of it just as the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation on it is one.
And if you break two bones together, the swelling is converted to all parts.
And if the more delicate bone is broken, the swelling is converted to the parts in front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, swelling appears below it, and it is from those causes that you are not aware of.
And immediately administer extension and alignment and binding of the splints accordingly.
But it is necessary, if the fracture of the bone is hard with many fragments, that the extension be lesser and more gradual, and rely on your skill.

And then wrap around each one a cloth in two layers according to its length, and place one at the end of the bone on one side and the other on the opposite side, and they should be from the end to the lower part of the body.

Chapter XVII.
About the fracture of the shinbone and the bones of the foot and the aforementioned bones.
A complete fracture does not usually occur in the shinbone; but it can happen to some extent with the bones of the foot. And for those bones of the foot, fractures are rare, and they do not typically suffer such issues except for minor injuries due to improper positioning.

And it is important for you not to forget that you should consider all the causes mentioned to you in other fractures and accidents that we have discussed; and prepare yourself for every accident with what is necessary.
Chapter XVIII.
Of the fracture of the jaw of a woman and of the bone of the penis and of the shaft of the penis in a man.

And immediately place poultices on its back, and when it wants to urinate, remove the gauze with ease and then let it urinate, and reduce it and return that according to the method by which you mixed it at the beginning, and then return it to its original poultice.

When handling the penis of a man when it is grasped, take a piece of leather and place the penis in it; then press upon its wrapping of cloth, and let it be wrapped up, and afterwards let it lie undisturbed for three days or thereabouts, and it will heal.
Chapter XIX.
On the restoration of fractured bones when they are out of place.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture out of place, and especially if the bone is large like that of the thigh or the arm, or similar to these, that you should cauterize him at once if the conditions for cauterization indicate it to you as we have said above.
And if the wound is bleeding, then you should proceed to stop the bleeding in this way: powder finely triturated substances over it, unless some other cause is present that prevents this.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this unless an abscess occurs.
But if an abscess occurs, then you must delay the restoration until the ninth day, waiting for the abscess to subside, and do not approach it on the third or fourth day thereafter, for you will cause worse accidents.
But if the broken bone protrudes above the skin, then you should proceed to reduce it and equalize it with your hands as easily and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the reduction of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length being seven or eight digits, and its width according to the quantity of the wound; and for this reason it is necessary for the surgeon to have three or four of these, according to what may be needed for this in all kinds of fractures.
And let it be round, and in little wrinkles because it should not be folded; place the compression upon that at the time of the operation.
And let it have a sharp point at which there is a margin in the veins' extremities; and let its upper part be thicker tending, and from the middle of it to the sides.
And this is the form of it:
And it is called in Greek "labrum voluntas atelarem," which means a small palette.
So that the end of it may be placed with the tip raised above the highest point of the bone.
And press it in one turn so that the bone returns to its place, and let it be even with some adjustment.
And thus strive to align the ends of the fracture between them, and if the end of the broken bone is slender, and does not accept that instrument well, then it is necessary that you insert the end of that bone so that the instrument has access to it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely as we have recommended, then insert it in such a way that is appropriate for one of the saws which we have mentioned; or cut it with one of the saws in whatever manner is possible for you.
And smooth that which remains on the bone of roughness and small scratches.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient still experiences severe pain in the tooth, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and thus do what will greatly benefit the patient.
When you have completed the restoration of the bone that you are performing, and it is now time to soak a cloth in black styptic wine, especially if it is summer, and do not apply cerote or any other substance containing oil on the wound, so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption.
And immediately administer splints at the time of completing the restoration of the bone, and leave the wound uncovered so that you can incise any protruding fragments with forceps and use bandages according to the extent of the wound.
And be extremely careful not to stain the wound with the fracture, for many times this has been done by careless surgeons, and it caused death or chronic illness in their patients, which is called sepsis. And if the swelling is slight, soft, and different from the swelling of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or it is a bad wound, and you fear some of the harmful accidents we have mentioned, and you feel pain in that area, then do not use splints at that time, but make a bandage with thick cloths instead of splints and apply it to the area.
And after one day or two, when the wound begins to generate pus, then you remove the cloth that you had placed over it with wine, and immediately administer medications, such as those commonly used to treat wounds, including tetramericon and similar remedies.
And it is necessary for you to untie the bandage and examine the wound every morning and evening until it is healed and sealed, and it should be prepared in such a way that putrefaction can flow away from it downward with ease.
If several days pass since the wound and it is not healed nor has it become detached from the flesh, then you should know that here you are dealing with small issues for which you must inquire about the condition of the wound by attempting to examine it. And of those issues, those that are separated by manual removal and extraction, those that cannot be separated and are left in the member causing pain, perforate them as you incise according to what is possible for you.
And if the wound becomes infected with gangrene or erysipelas, which are severe forms of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary for you to pause and choose one of those methods according to what is appropriate for the treatment, as previously described in its chapter.
And concerning that in which it is necessary for me to speak and you should present your hand: when the bone is broken, treat the limb as you would treat the shell of an egg or those similar to large bones, and do not assume traction on it nor its extraction because more than two or three times this might lead to death; leave that inside so that it can heal, for within about twenty days or thirty, it will likely fall out by itself. And then treat the wound if you see fit, and if not, leave it as is.
Chapter XX.
On the treatment of knots that occur in the healing of some fracture.
This knot often occurs in the healing process of a fracture, especially near the joints, and it distorts the shape of the limb; and it significantly hinders the natural function of the limb.
Then consider whether the swelling is recent and administer medicines that have a styptic effect such as aloes, oak bark, iris root, myrrh, acacia gum, and similar substances; take some or all of these. Mix them with white vinegar or apple cider vinegar or any agreeable vinegar, and apply this over the swelling on lint, and bind it securely over it with good compression.
And let there be strict bandaging so that it is not loosened for several days; then loosen it and reapply another one, until the swelling subsides; or place a lead plate over it gently, since lead has the property of reducing whatever is inflamed in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned into a hard lump and it is necessary to remove it immediately due to its severity, then cut it at its most dominant part, and excise the excess or reduce it with some remedies until it subsides; and treat it until it heals.
Chapter XXI.
On the restoration of fractures when they are set and subsequently the limb is moved gently, not contrary to its natural motion.
When a bone fracture is set and subsequently the limb is moved gently, it is done for various reasons: among which one reason is excessive soaking of the limb and bandaging not according to what is needed; or by excess tightness in the bindings that prohibit proper nutrition from reaching the limb; or by excessive embrocation; or by unnecessary movement outside its proper time; or due to insufficient blood in the patient's body and weakness.
For the treatment of this condition, give food to the patient and make their heart tender so that blood may be multiplied within them; administer baths; and apply wax and myrrh to it.
And apply plasters to the limb, so that the plaster draws out the nourishment, or continue rubbing with cold water lotion, until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter XXII.
On the treatment of broken bones when they have healed improperly and prohibit further operation on them as is necessary.
When it happens that a limb which has already been restored near the healing of its curvature and prominence in the broken bone and knotting, and this is due to the deformation of the shape of the limb, but since the limb is not prohibited from its natural movement, you do not need to accept the word of one who believes that member is newly broken.
But now such false physicians and restorers exist who have done this in our land; and this operation is a disgraceful one leading to great fear, the sign of which is fatal.
But if the limping and knotting are recent, then it is necessary to rub them with cold water lotion in which you soak softening herbs such as chamomile, rosemary, and melilot, and similarly prepared plasters for softening should be applied; or take mucilage of rosemary and mix it with linseed meal or olive oil, and apply it as a plaster; or take fat figs and grind them with dove's milk, and similar medicines that are called consolidating remedies.
And in some cases it is dissolved by constant gentle friction which is applied to the most humid parts and movement of the member is administered to all parts at all times.
If tortuosity has already advanced and become hard and has been converted into stone and has caused the need for treatment with iron, then one must let the pus be discharged and the continuation of the bone be dissolved, and cut away what is superfluous from the nodes of that tissue with sharp instruments, and facilitate this by careful attention and study.
Chapter XXIII.
On the topic of speech regarding dislocation.
Dislocation is the misalignment of any of the joints from its proper place, by which movement is prohibited, and the figure of the member is distorted, causing severe pain and great injury.
When a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to attempt its reduction as soon as possible and not delay in doing so, for if it is delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and hard, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And for this reason it is not advisable to move or keep the patient standing during the period of inflammation, as movement often causes spasms and severe pain.
However, when this occurs, it is necessary to prepare for the incision of the affected area, and allow time for the inflammation to subside; then the limb should be bathed with hot water and oil, and afterward reduction should be attempted with care.
And all treatment of the member should be done with what will help it to return to its proper place.
Here are the bones set in order as they were described for fractures; I now enter into the part concerning lesser wounds.
Chapter XXIIIV.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the lower jaw.
When both jaws are dislocated and if they do not reduce by themselves; the dislocation of both can be done in one of two ways: either they are partially out of place and somewhat relaxed; or they are completely dislocated and fully displaced, such that they are relaxed at the joint where saliva flows from the mouth and cannot close it, and the tongue is wrapped within the mouth.
But when the dislocation is slight, it often returns on its own with little cause.
But if the dislocation is complete and thorough, it must be reduced promptly and not delayed at all.
And it is that the minister hold the head of the patient, and the physician insert the thumb of one hand into the joint of the dislocated jaw inside the mouth if the dislocation is on one side; or he inserts the thumbs of both hands together if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers should be outside equally; and commands the patient to relax their jaws and allow them to move apart from each side; and the physician then guides the jaw and presses it until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer evaporation with hot water and oil until the reduction can be made gently for both, and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And thus when returning to the bandages, and the mouth of the wound is closed but not softened, then powder from linen soaked in wax and rose oil should be applied, and it should be laid on with ease using soft cloths.
And let the patient sleep on their side, and their head between their two ears, so that they do not turn to either the right or left; and do not force them to chew for any reason; have their food cut up finely until such time as when the pains are diminished and their jaws well healed, then allow them to eat what they wish.
And this should be administered with ease, and do not work on the opening of the mouth for eating and drinking and speaking until such time as the jaw is healed and cured.
And if the reduction of both jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at some point and not returned to their proper places, then fevers often occur along with persistent sweat, and very easily the patient's belly becomes swollen, and frequently vomiting occurs with pure bile.
And when you see this, then you know that it is lost; and very often the one to whom this happens dies within x days.
Chapter XXV.

Chapter XXVI.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is when it is dislocated at the upper part of the muscle, and this happens slowly; and it is not dislocated at the rear parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the front parts by the nerves.
And it is most commonly dislocated and comes out at the lower parts below the elbow, and more easily in those whose muscles are thin; but in those with thick muscles, it comes out with difficulty.
If it happens that some person is struck or falls, and the muscle of the limb becomes inflamed with inflammation, and is cut due to what has already been dislocated.
Then you should apply what is inside to the area that is swollen over it, and now proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation of it is known when it is in the lower parts of the joint; therefore, you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the normal one, for you notice obvious differences among them; you find a depression at the head of the muscle, and below the joint, the touch of the muscle's head feels like that of a finger, and the injured person cannot return their hand to its usual position, nor can they move it easily.
If it is dislocated below the foot or above, then you will immediately notice a clear touch, and it is not concealed.

Chapter XXVII.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Neck.
Know that the joint of the neck is dislocated with difficulty and seems to return with difficulty; and it can be dislocated in all directions, but mostly forward or backward. The dislocation is not concealed from you as when it lies below the vision or touch according to some figure it has taken, and when you adjust the dislocated neck, this is clearly shown to you because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm nor reach with it its own muscle.
And it is necessary that you treat the neck, and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it becomes an abscess due to heat, because if it reaches a hot abscess, reducing it will be difficult, and even if healed completely, recovery will be very slow; especially when it affects the rear parts, for it is worse than all other types of dislocations, causing greater pain, and many times death results from it.
And the restoration of it is that which can be reduced, is that the hand should be extended across two boards, and the arm should be expanded, and the hands of the physician should be on his shoulder and below it, and he presses the joint with the tips of his fingers together, or with the palm of his hand until it returns to its place.
If the dislocation is to the front parts, then it returns thus that he bends the hand with his hand, until he strikes the shoulder with the tips of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer vigorous extension and strong force.
And it is that two ministers extend the arm, and hold it thus with two ministers below so that it is not raised due to the extension.
And subsequently the arm should be rotated from all sides, since it is wrapped in folded cloth long over his hand, or bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this be those causes which help in the lubricity of the joint for ease; and then press the joint with strong pressure until it returns.
And then the reduction should be such that he rests on a plaster which is styptic and supportive on a pillow of wool, and is bandaged with moderate tightness, and the arm is suspended from the neck, and he is left for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and thus if the joint is formed in its place, then you loosen the bandage of him and leave it.
And if you see that the joint should not be stitched together, then apply the plaster and bandage again, and leave it like that for several days until it is healed; then later untie it.
And if it happens that there is swelling around the joint reduction and its disposition is in motion, then administer moistening with baths and gentle friction, and compression until it subsides; or place on the joint something that restrains movement, which is a matter of heavy oil; and then bind it, and leave it like that day and night. And then remove that, and put it in a bath; and when it is warm, then you should rub the joint with moderate friction, and then apply something that restrains movement, which is a salve for heavy oil, once, and the second time, and the third time, [and so on]. in the bath instrument, until it is healed.
And if you wish, place on it pieces of boiled cowhide that have been softened in water and attach them to it; and do this several times, as it is moist and helps to reduce its disposition.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the elbow joint, which is between the arm and the hand.
The dislocation of the hand sometimes occurs, and the reduction of such a dislocation is easy, but the opposite is true for other joints. However, it is necessary to act with speed in reducing it at the hour when it happens, before swelling sets in or an abscess forms.
