And if it returns immediately, that is good; but if not, then strengthen that area with a bandage over the abscess and leave it like that; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, it will neither be endured nor can you proceed with the reduction of the pus on subsequent days.
However, the joint moves according to its tortuousness; and if you notice nothing in the patient except that the hand is softened and cannot be strengthened for any cause; then you know that the nerves are torn or severed, and therefore do not proceed but apply cauterization near the affected area and strengthen it with something else that confers a cause.
When it has been returned properly, then place the bandage we have recommended on it, and let it be left and strengthened for five days; and afterwards, it should be loosened and left, with the hand being exercised.
However, if the movement of it is restricted and you encounter any gravity in it, then moisten that with warm water and friction several times until it is alleviated.
Chapter XXIX.
On the treatment of the aforementioned dislocation.
Sometimes these are dislocated completely.
When a finger is dislocated at the joint near the palm or at the base, hold the finger steady and push back the dislocation with your fingers until it returns.
And then hold the tip of the finger, and suspend that above the part where it is dislocated, and leave it like this for two days.
And then suspend that and extend it until it is properly bandaged on that day.
And when it is night, wrap that according to the narration, and do not stop unwrapping it during the day and exercising with movements, and wrap it at night; and do this for several days until it is strong.
And do the same for this if it is dislocated at the base of the hand, and wrap it in that part and perform the first operation like this until it is healed.
And do the same for this when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter XXX.
On the treatment of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation occurs to any of the vertebrae of the back or neck and they move from their places, then there is no treatment for this, as death is feared for the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's excretion occurs without their will, and they cannot control it; and often some parts of them become weak or two sides of them or both arms, or one of these; or a vertebra is displaced from its place, as sometimes it is displaced and sometimes removed to four directions.
Thus it is described for the parts behind and is called jellyfish-like.
The treatment for this is to consider whether the condition arose from childbirth, because if it did, there is no cure and no healing from that.
But for the case that arises from falling or being struck or similar circumstances, the Ancients described the methods of treatment at length in their speeches, much of which I cannot avoid repeating; but I have abbreviated what I can, to excuse myself from excesses, because of my proximity to understanding and my exposition, with which I have made this clear and formed it from various instruments different from those by which I show this and demonstrate that.
And none of those is cured except the one that occurs specifically as I mention.
And it is that the patient lie on his face near a wall, and be placed beneath a canopy moistened so that his feet are not exposed.
And near by a straight post should be placed, planted in the ground at the end near his head, and another post around his feet at the other end of the bed.
And the minister should hold one post, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another minister should hold the other according to that form.
And nearby, wrap around the head of the patient and below the shoulders a firm linen band; extend the end of the band to the post that is behind his head, and tie it there; then stretch the other band over the hips near the armpits and behind the thighs; then adjust all the bands and tie them to the other post that is behind his feet.
Then the minister shall extend the bandage from the end of the stump, and let not the two stumps and their respective bandages be moved from the places in which they are fixed, but rather let them not be made too tight as we have said.
Then the physician shall place his palms on the strong part of the spine until it returns, or he should place it on that table, and then adjust it on the table until it returns.
But if it does not return by this method, now take a table whose width or length is about three arms, and dig into the wall near the patient a place where the end of the table can fit. Then place the middle of the table on the spine, and the physician shall stand with his weight on the other end of the table, and carefully do so until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open the ring which is revolved around the hand; and it is that you place behind the patient's head at the end of two pieces, the length of each being one arm, and behind the other part of the body similarly two pieces; and let the distance between each piece be a palm, and attach them firmly to the ground so they do not move.
And there should be one round piece in which the ring is placed, and it should be revolved around the two ends of the two pieces.
And at the end of it there is an end piece in which the round piece is formed, the length of which is a palm where it is revolved; and on the two other pieces there is something similar to that.
Take the bandages that you have tightly on the patient's feet in the place where you are to cut off the head of them, and the bandages that you have tightly on the calves of them in the turn where you are to cut off the feet.
And immediately each turn should have a minister who holds the turn with his hand, and the surgeon likewise twists the spine as we have said.
And after the spine has been returned and the place is adjusted, now it is necessary to apply an excrescent plaster on a piece of cloth, and then cover with cotton; and afterwards place over the plaster strips of wood whose width should be three fingers or about that, and the length should take up the area affected by the inflammation, and around the part of the spine.
And then wrap with the bandage that we have mentioned, and let the patient use delicate nutrition until he is healed.
And if any inflammation remains in the area at the end of the healing process, now it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and alleviate over a long period as we have recommended; and already a sheet of lead has been applied to that place.
And sometimes inflammation occurs in the back of the vertebrae of the spine, and it is caused by that dislocation; and that bone is one for which no precaution should be taken with this medicine, because it could easily lead to death.
Chapter XXXI.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the attachment of the muscle suffer only from dislocation alone, and do not suffer what affects other joints with slight displacement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated in four ways, for it can be dislocated inwardly, outwardly, forward, or backward.
And it is most commonly dislocated inwardly, and rarely forward or backward.
And the sign of a dislocation inward is that when you adjust the leg of the patient on the affected side compared to the healthy side, it is longer and the heel is more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their leg towards their chin, and this area is where an abscess would be cauterized, so the head of the bone has already reached here.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs outwardly is that the symptoms are contrary to those mentioned for an inward dislocation.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs forward is that when they try to straighten their leg, it does not bend without pain in the heel, and if they attempt to move, they cannot do so forward; and their urine is retained and their breathing is constricted, and they walk on their toes.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs backward is that they cannot extend their heel, nor can they bend it forward without pain in breathing, and their leg is shorter than the other, and their breathing is relaxed, and the head of the bone is located there.
The final reduction of the types of this dislocation is that you consider, at this time, if the dislocation is old and ancient, which already has become accustomed to being in that place, you administer reduction gently according to its disposition; now it is not entirely in that cure; and then it does not need to be made a matter of precaution.
In the case of one in which the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four types of dislocation, then you should attempt to return the joint and extend it in its internal and external parts, and move it from side to side, for it easily returns without needing further treatment.
However, if it does not return in this manner, then you should prepare a strong assistant and extend the limb from that part below, or with its muscles, or with a bandage which is placed over the limb above the joint; and another assistant extends the hand of that one from above so that he places his hands below the ribs; and then tightly bind a lion's skin over the affected area, and have another assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And this extension of the limb or of the part internally towards the angle of the shoulder blade, or of the rear part towards the side.
And perform the extension of all of these at once while the patient is raised up on his back and remains suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four types.
And so if the dislocation returns as we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for all types to proceed with what I will describe regarding its specific treatment.
In the reduction of the shoulder, when it is dislocated and the dislocation is towards the inner parts, the patient must lie supine on his back; then place the bandages along the ridge of the shoulder at the location between the top of the shoulder and the area below the collarbone; then extend the bandage from the armpit to the farthest part of the clavicle.
And then another strong minister should take hold of his arms and forcefully press the thick part of the dislocated shoulder outward with great extension, for that returns it to its place.
And this particular case is easier to cure than the other cases once this member is reduced.
But if it is excused upon you and you do not obey the regimen for this type of treatment completely, then the patient's two sides should be bandaged together tightly with a strong, light bandage on both thighs and both arms.
And the length of each band should be four fingers wide, and the affected side should be extended more than the other by two fingers.
And then the patient should be suspended above the head of the post, and he should be hanging; and the distance from him to the ground should be two arm lengths.
And then command a strong youth to press the top of the shoulder, and have another youth hold the patient, while this other youth presses the shoulder forcefully, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in the reduction of the shoulder, when the dislocation is towards the outer part, the patient should lie on his side as we have recommended for the golden position; and a strong bandage should be applied to the affected side properly and over his feet.
By the poles should you place the sticks, one at the feet and the other at the head; and near them should you place sticks adjusted to the side of the hill placed upon which wet cloths should wrap around, so that the patient not be harmed by the stick being among the things of him, so that from the extension to the parts below it not be pulled.
And near each minister should extend to his part, and the doctor with his hand applies the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is to the front parts, then it is necessary that the bench be extended quickly, and according to this method described on the hill; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand on the patient's side, and nearby grasps with the other hand, and with that grasp extends the one stretched out to the lower part towards the front.
And if the dislocation is to the rear parts, then it is not necessary for the patient to be stretched out to the lower part and raised above the ground; rather, it should be placed on a hard surface, as it should also be done if the hip joint is dislocated outwardly, according to what we have said about lying on two planks; and it is on his back and the limbs are extended, as we have said, there is no harm.
And it is necessary to administer compression on the table at the location where the joint is.
Thus, when the reduction is complete as required — and the sign of the reduction being successful is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's limb in two directions, and when you see those angles and the patient extends their joint and expands it for the sake of comfort, then you know that the limb has returned to its proper position as needed — and then you adjust two things and pause before applying bandages such that the width is not compromised in any part; and adhere the patient to rest for three days or four.
And then loosen the bandage and the dressing, and compare one limb to the other.
Now, if you see those angles properly, then you know that the dislocation has already been fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any sign of swelling, then you return and bandage that area, and extend it according to your initial structure, and let it be for three days.
And then loosen that area, and have the patient delay in walking on it for several days until it is comfortable.
Chapter XXXII.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Shoulder.
The shoulder can be dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated outward, inward, and downward toward the back; it cannot be dislocated forward.
And the sign of shoulder dislocation is that when you instruct the patient to apply their hand to the head, then you know that the shoulder is dislocated.
And for the restoration of all manners of the thing that you should do to the patient straightaway, namely that you extend his leg if possible; adhere your hand to his face; place his foot between your thighs; and then adhere your fingers around the handle, and hold those firmly around the handle with strength, and another minister extends his leg so that the bone returns to its place.
And the sign of recovery for the patient is that his leg adheres to the body without laborious effort.
And then apply a plaster to that and make the leg adhere to the body, and then bind those together with a bandage for three days or four, and then loosen them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days until he is healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction of it by you is unnecessary, now administer strong extension with bandages, as described in the preceding narration about the treatment of the hip.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the thigh.
The thigh in some cases is displaced slightly by minor movement, and in other cases it is dislocated according to the degree, and the dislocation of it occurs either towards the inner parts or towards the outer parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the thigh swollen towards the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its displacement is easy recovery, and it should be extended with ease while massaging until it returns to its place.
In the treatment of the thigh when it is dislocated for the second time, make sure to lay the patient straight, and let him have a strong and capable attendant behind each of his two thighs in their proper place.
And place yourself near the patient with your right hand above the upper part of the thigh, and your left hand below the thigh at the location of the joint.
And draw the foot towards you with your right hand, and hold it with your left hand for two moments, then extend your left hand; and press the soles of the feet with your right hand vigorously.
And do this two times as we have counted.
And then press the soles of the foot with your hand for the third time, and you will be at the joint.
Therefore, if it returns in one attempt or two following this method, and if the foot becomes straightened, it is good; and otherwise, immediately repeat the operation, as it will return.
But if it is prohibited for you to reduce it as we have recommended, then make the patient lie on their back on the ground, and roll a sheet into a firm roller, which should fit between the thighs, and you should wrap the patient in a cloth so that they do not move.
And then the attendant holding the patient's thighs should do so firmly, and another attendant should extend the foot with his hands or with a bandage placed around the ankle; and then each attendant should pull against the extension on their side, and the rolled sheet should be extended between the patient’s thighs, restraining them, so that the body of the patient is not pulled towards the parts below during the extension.
Here is the English translation:
And when the dislocation returns and the condition of its reduction is clear to you, apply a plaster and bandage it tightly with a strong cloth, and bind the limb to the splint.
And be careful to protect the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage over them is not too tight and does not injure them severely.
And leave it like that for two days or three; and if the bandage is loosened, redress it.
And then untie it on the third day or the fourth, and prohibit the patient from moving for forty days. But if the patient is forced to move before that time, there is no assurance that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted, and thus it will not receive proper treatment.
And if an abscess requires cauterization, then you should proceed according to the instructions given in our narrative regarding the treatment and application, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Said Ankle.
Make sure that the one who is dislocated rests with little extension without labor; and this is not difficult, but rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in some joints of the two feet, then make the patient lie on his back on a flat surface or table, and have him positioned as if he were sitting.
And then stand on your foot, and place what you intend to realign over the joints, and step on it firmly with your foot until it returns to its proper position, and see that it is now aligned and does not appear in a prominent location.
After the pause, place the foot on a board that can hold the entire foot, which has two ends, and tightly bind it with a proper and firm bandage for three days; then loosen that binding and allow it to rest from walking for fourteen days, until it is strong and its healing is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
About the kinds of dislocation that are made due to injury, fracture, or any combination thereof.
When any of those causes occur, and you fail in the treatment of it and in its restoration, many times death follows.
And because of this, one should not presume to treat it unless they are a wise practitioner with long experience, merciful and pious, neither hasty nor presumptuous; and in the beginning, use medicines that only reduce swelling, and leave the patient's fate to divine judgment, but seek out the one to whom you hope for salvation from perdition, or relief from illness, where there appears any hope. Then strive to reduce it as soon as possible at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes swollen and severe.
And then if the limb returns as we wish, immediately administer a regimen that reduces swelling and treats the injury with what is appropriate from among dry applications.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and occurs in bones that are separated, then you must manage its treatment as we have described for simple injuries, dealing with it in the locations where such conditions arise.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that of the instrument in the ways of fear as it is preceded by my preaching; for this is better for you to serve your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and most healthful for your true body you will see these in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which God be blessed. Amen.
